/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Alas, each and every one of those pitches were either rejected or (by and large) ignored.</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>Ironically, the email that finally worked was one line sent straight to Arianna Huffington herself…</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://i2.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-aaron3.png?ssl=1\" alt=\"Successful guest posting pitch email sent to Arianna Huffington\" class=\"wp-image-24672\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-aaron3.png?w=1999&#38;ssl=1 1999w, https://i2.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-aaron3.png?resize=150%2C62&#38;ssl=1 150w, https://i2.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-aaron3.png?resize=300%2C123&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i2.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-aaron3.png?resize=768%2C315&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-aaron3.png?resize=800%2C329&#38;ssl=1 800w, https://i2.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-aaron3.png?resize=990%2C407&#38;ssl=1 990w, https://i2.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-aaron3.png?resize=1320%2C542&#38;ssl=1 1320w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Finally, success:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Arianna-Huffington-response.png?ssl=1\" alt=\"Response from Arianna Huffington to Aaron Orendorff's guest posting pitch email\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Not only is that the most successful template I’ve ever used, but the illustrious <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://www.orbitmedia.com/team/andy-crestodina/\" target=\"_blank\">Andy Crestodina</a> also immortalized its ethos in his book, <em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Content-Chemistry-Illustrated-Handbook-Marketing/dp/0988336464\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\">Content Chemistry</a></em>, as well as blowing up my social accounts every few months by including it in a number of his conference presentations:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Kelvin-Claveria.png?ssl=1\" alt=\"\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/><figcaption><em>Image via </em><a href=\"https://twitter.com/kcclaveria/status/1034603380746805253\"><em>Kelvin Claveria (Twitter)</em></a></figcaption></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>But, what if you’re not a writer?</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 class=\"has-text-align-center\">Guest posting myth 3: Guest posts only work for ‘content creators’</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>There are plenty of objections to adopting guest posting as a growth strategy for your business. Most of them &#8211; to be honest &#8211; are excuses to shield us from the risk of rejection.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The most-plausible sounding protest goes like this:</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>“Sure, guest blogging worked for you, Aaron. But you’re a writer. I’m not. It won’t work for me.”</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>Lies! But don’t take my word for it.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Rather than try to convince you that guest posting can add fuel to any career path or service… instead, I asked 35 influencer-status leaders in a variety of industries three questions:</p>\n\n\n\n<ol><li>Has guest blogging helped you grow your business?</li><li>How has guest blogging helped grow your business?</li><li>What’s the one thing you wish you knew when you started?</li></ol>\n\n\n\n<p>Here’s the best part…</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>20 of the 35 were not writers or content creators!</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>Using an incredibly scientific scale, here’s how their answers to the first question about “has” guest blogging helped shook out…</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li><strong>Hell yeah!</strong> 63%</li><li><strong>Yes:</strong> 26%</li><li><strong>Sorta:</strong> 11%</li><li><strong>No:</strong> 0%</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>You can  <a href=\"\" data-leadbox-popup=\"1450f2973f72a2:17f2c5a05b46dc\" data-leadbox-domain=\"copyhackers.lpages.co\">grab</a> all 35 answers to the second two questions, so &#8211; for now &#8211; I simply want to highlight three of my favorite responses from two non-writers and one content creator.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>First up: Savannah Sanchez, a paid media manager at the Facebook agency <a href=\"https://commonthreadco.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\">Common Thread Collective</a>. Earlier this year, Savannah published her first guest post. The result?</p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Savannah-Sanchez.png?ssl=1\" alt=\"Savannah Sanchez on guest blogging\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/><figcaption>Savannah Sanchez, Common Thread Collective</figcaption></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>“After guest blogging on Sumo, I was able to generate a significant increase in leads and traffic to <a href=\"http://thesocialsavannah.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\">my website</a>, social following and Common Thread Collective.</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>“It was a huge win all around &#8211; and it never hurts to have your boss see business come in from your offsite efforts.</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>“From there, I’ve often referenced my guest blogging when submitting to speaking opportunities. Plus, it’s been a great bonus to include the article on my personal website to add more validation of my expertise.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“Last, my guest blog is also ranking highly for the “Facebook Metrics” keyword, which is an awesome spot to be in (especially heading into the <a href=\"https://commonthreadco.com/blogs/coachs-corner/black-friday-social-media-campaigns\">holiday’s peak campaign season</a>).”</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Her advice to would-be (and soon-to-be!) guest bloggers is equally illuminating:</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>“Keep engaged with the comments! It’s easy to forget to comment moderate when the blog doesn’t live on your own website.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“However, there could be valuable potential clients that are asking questions in the comments of your guest blog.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Second, Ross Simmonds &#8211; CEO of <a href=\"https://foundationinc.co/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\">Foundation Marketing</a>. You may know Ross from his top-rated conference appearances, his prolific Twitter output or his writing.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>But, Ross is <em>not</em> a writer by trade. Instead, he’s essentially a CMO (chief marketing officer) for hire who majors on strategy, ecommerce operations and video marketing:</p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Ross-Simmonds.png?ssl=1\" alt=\"Ross Simmonds, Foundation Marketing, on guest posting\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/><figcaption>Ross Simmonds, Foundation Marketing</figcaption></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>“Guest blogging was the fastest way for me early on in my career to build relationships with some of the best in the industry, build an audience, establish authority and drive more traffic to my site.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“As I began writing for bigger publications, more opportunities unlocked and with every opportunity came the chance to connect with a new audience. It’s one of the most effective ways to reach an already engaged and targeted audience.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>His tip on how to “drive additional eyeballs to existing content that you have developed” stopped me in my tracks:</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“One of the key drivers of our agency’s early success on Slideshare was the act of embedding our decks into guest posts.</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>“This resulted in generating more than 1M views on Slideshare and generating thousands of emails.”</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>Third, <a href=\"https://www.joshsteimle.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\" (opens in a new tab)\">Josh Steimle</a> &#8211; our <em>token </em>writer. <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/12.0.0-1/72x72/1f609.png\" alt=\"😉\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Josh is the author of <em>The 7 Systems of Influence</em> and Founder of <a href=\"https://www.mwi.com/\">MWI International Digital Agency</a>. Pay special attention to his careful selection of sites to publish on and how that correlates with results:</p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Josh-Steimle.png?ssl=1\" alt=\"Josh Steimle, MWI International Digital Agency, on guest posting\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/><figcaption>Josh Steimle, MWI International Digital Agency</figcaption></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>“I’ve written 300+ articles for more than two dozen top-tier publications like Forbes, Entrepreneur and Mashable. But &#8211; when it comes to building a personal brand &#8211; guest blogging for smaller, niche sites has been a necessary ingredient to give me ‘street cred.’</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>“That enhanced credibility has led to more revenue for my companies, speaking gigs and a book deal.</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>“For example, when I went to launch my masterclass on how to become a contributor, guest blogging on <a href=\"https://problogger.com/how-a-simple-blog-led-to-writing-for-forbes-mashable-and-techcrunch-2/\">ProBlogger</a> allowed me to get in front of my ideal audience where they were hanging out and in a more credible way than if the same post had been in <em>Time</em> or <em>Inc</em>.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>What did he wish he knew before he got started?</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>“Don’t use impersonal templates for your pitches with a cluttered list of ideas! Any website worth guest blogging on gets hundreds if not thousands of pitches per month and they can spot a template pitch a mile away.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“The worst offender I see starts with: ‘I see you post about [topic]. I really liked your post [post title]. I would like to contribute a guest post on this topic at no cost to you, just include a link to our website.’</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>“Instead of doing what everyone else is doing, playing the guest blog lottery and hoping you win by sending out a lot of emails, send fewer emails but increase the quality.”</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>“First, verify that they accept guest blog posts… Second, if they do accept guest posts, write a paragraph or two that don’t merely prove you know what they’ve already published but show you know where they’re going and what they want to publish in the future.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“Think of a blog owner as a collector of rare animals and find what’s missing from their collection.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“If someone came to me and said, ‘I see you’re focusing a lot these days on your 7 Systems, but you haven&#8217;t published a lot about System #3 and I have some ideas for guest posts that could give you more content focused on that,’ then that would be pretty compelling.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“It would prove to me they’ve gone further than a temple and they really know the content on my site. Who knows, perhaps someone could even convince me to start accepting guest posts with a pitch like that.”</p>\n\n\n\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Want even more guidance and inspiration? </strong> <a data-leadbox-popup=\"1450f2973f72a2:17f2c5a05b46dc\" data-leadbox-domain=\"copyhackers.lpages.co\" href=\"\">Then grab all 35 of the original contributions here…</a><br></p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Screen-Shot-2019-11-27-at-2.21.27-PM.png?ssl=1\" alt=\"PDF download from Aaron Orendorrf 'Authority &#38; the Benefits of Guest Blogging'.\" class=\"wp-image-24743\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Screen-Shot-2019-11-27-at-2.21.27-PM.png?w=656&#38;ssl=1 656w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Screen-Shot-2019-11-27-at-2.21.27-PM.png?resize=116%2C150&#38;ssl=1 116w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Screen-Shot-2019-11-27-at-2.21.27-PM.png?resize=232%2C300&#38;ssl=1 232w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 656px) 100vw, 656px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<script src=\"//static.leadpages.net/leadboxes/current/embed.js\" async=\"\" defer=\"\"></script> <a href=\"\" data-leadbox-popup=\"1450f2973f72a2:17f2c5a05b46dc\" data-leadbox-domain=\"copyhackers.lpages.co\">Download the BONUS: Authority &#38; the Benefits of Guest Blogging (PDF)</a>\n\n\n\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n\n\n\n<h2 class=\"has-text-align-center\">One last (BONUS!) guest posting myth: The truth is #LetsGetRejected</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Finally, the last and perhaps most dangerous myth about guest posting is: “No means it’s over.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Let me assure you of two things.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Number one: rejection is coming. Number two: rejection is just the beginning.</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>I’d wager 75% of all my published articles were rejected by at least one outlet (often more than one).</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>The keyword in that last sentence? Published.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Within the course, I pull back the curtain on the reality of rejection and share how<em> those same articles &#8211; with slight tweaks &#8211; got almost immediately approved by a different site</em>.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>In fact, to show I’m serious, this is what the true behind-the-scenes history of my first mainstream article &#8211; the one about Mindy Kaling &#8211; looked like:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?ssl=1\" alt=\"Email history of guest post pitches by Aaron Orendorff\" class=\"wp-image-24673\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?w=2404&#38;ssl=1 2404w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?resize=150%2C133&#38;ssl=1 150w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?resize=300%2C267&#38;ssl=1 300w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?resize=768%2C682&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?resize=800%2C711&#38;ssl=1 800w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?resize=990%2C880&#38;ssl=1 990w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?resize=1320%2C1173&#38;ssl=1 1320w, https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?w=2000&#38;ssl=1 2000w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>I’d sent that exact same email not only to seven different addresses at Entrepreneur… but a week prior I’d also sent it to multiple addresses at:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>Huffington Post</li><li>Mashable</li><li>Forbes</li><li>Inc.</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>Each of those pubs either rejected it or didn’t respond to the same f****** article. Seventeen nos. But, it only takes one yes.</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>With every article I wrote, every pitch I sent, every pitch I resent… and resent &#8211; as my finger hovered over the trackpad… my cursor atop the scariest button you’ll ever see in your life &#8211; “Send” &#8211; every time, I told myself:</p>\n\n\n\n<p>“Let’s get rejected.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Why?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Because I knew that fear was going to be the thing that stopped me. Fear of hearing no. Fear of hearing nothing. Fear of being told, “This isn’t good enough. You’re not good enough.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>And so, I embraced the fear. I rigged my heart and mind &#8211; I steeled myself against the terror &#8211; in the only way I knew how.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>I made rejection the goal.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>I wanted to be the kind of person who does shit. Fear was and is the only thing that stops me &#8211; that stops any of us &#8211; from doing really, really, really great shit.</p>\n\n\n\n<h5><em>Embracing the oncoming failure inverts it. It turns fear on itself.</em></h5>\n\n\n\n<p>If you’d like to join me on the journey, you can sign up for the course <a href=\"https://copyhackers.com/content-school-get-on-the-list\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\"here (opens in a new tab)\">here</a>. Just remember: #LetsGetRejected.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Please, tell me when you do.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>And… when you don’t.</p>\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n\nwindow.rmpanda = window.rmpanda || .com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Josh-Steimle.png?ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn57@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-39-05-691_388e35e89e42660b.webp","https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Screen-Shot-2019-11-27-at-2.21.27-PM.png?ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn54@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-39-04-118_317fba70af05066d.webp","https://i1.wp.com/copyhackers.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/email-example-1.png?ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn70@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-39-02-746_bf3d277c9fca5cd9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598323135046},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:38:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:38:55","title":"Breakthrough-or-bust emails","link":"https://copyhackers.com/?p=24784","description":"\n<p>Best practices create mediocrity. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>That’s my take, at least. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>It’s also Dilbert’s take, I’ve just discovered: </p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577820221664_AML-17726_2988431_mutable_color.gif\" alt=\"Dilbert comic about mediocrity and best practices. Used with valid license for artwork. All rights reserved.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Something performs well a thousand years ago or twelve days ago, and it becomes a better practice. The repeated use of it turns it into a best practice &#8211; folks start to see it everywhere and think, Well, that’s gotta be THE way to do X. And then they start spreading that best practice in some part of their world, whether that’s within a five-person marketing department or across a 2000-person audience at an event or in an evergreen blog post that could see hundreds of thousands of visitors over the years… and voila. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Mediocrity is born. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Unintentionally. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The intent was to do better. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The effect was to shortcut thinking and normalize unchallenged marketing. To give us all something to say when we see copy, design, interfaces, etc that make us feel something: </p>\n\n\n\n<p>“But isn’t a best practice X?” </p>\n\n\n\n<p>(read: “This work challenges what I believe keeps me safe.”)</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The designer or copywriter goes back to the drawing board. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And mediocrity gets reinforced.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Mostly, we want to feel nothing when it comes to marketing we’re going to approve, where we&#8217;ll be held responsible for it. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Sure, we respond best to <em>other people&#8217;s </em>marketing that moves us, like:</p>\n\n\n\n<iframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/GUVkO6ts2pA\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\"></iframe>\n\n\n\n<p>and</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577827603431_diamond+shreddies.jpg\" alt=\"\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>and</p>\n\n\n\n<iframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/ilarNBQHevA\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\"></iframe>\n\n\n\n<p>and</p>\n\n\n\n<iframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/7-VvFlwsWic\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\"></iframe>\n\n\n\n<p><br>But when it comes to the marketing &#8211; and copy &#8211; we’re signing off on… well, when’s the last time you published something that made you feel something and, in doing so, made you uncomfortable that it would make others feel something and they might not like that you made them feel something? How often do you instead publish work that makes you feel safe, safe and only safe?</p>\n\n\n\n<h2>This past spring, we wrote a SaaS onboarding sequence that ticked all the boxes when it comes to email best practices. It made us all feel safe.</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>The email sequence in question was for <a href=\"https://prezi.com/\">Prezi</a>, the leading presentation platform for non-linear storytelling. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The sequence we wrote was filled to ye olde brim with best practices in email marketing and SaaS user onboarding. Here’s an example of one of the emails we wrote, in its designed form &#8211; see if you can&#8217;t spot the best practices at play:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1574970550461_CopyHackers_day2.png\" alt=\"Image of design-heavy email, as example for case study.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Did you identify the email best practices?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>It’s got everything all the “best practices” blogs, including in many cases <a href=\"https://copyhackers.com/2019/09/writing-ecommerce-emails/\">our blog</a>, say:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li><a href=\"https://blogs.constantcontact.com/email-design-best-practices/\">Branding to match user expectations and increase a feeling of safety</a></li><li><a href=\"https://blogs.constantcontact.com/how-to-write-better-headlines/\">A short, clear headline that is relevant to the reader</a></li><li><a href=\"https://sendpulse.com/blog/use-of-images-in-emails\">A good image in the first 500 px that does not distract from the message</a></li><li><a href=\"https://sendpulse.com/blog/use-of-images-in-emails\">Not too many images, though</a> </li><li><a href=\"https://neilpatel.com/blog/the-progress-bar/\">A progress bar / completeness meter (because the idea is that people like to finish things)</a></li><li><a href=\"https://www.mailpoet.com/blog/long-vs-short-why-you-should-keep-your-emails-brief/\">Enough copy but not too much copy</a> (some of the other emails in the sequence had more; some had less)</li><li><a href=\"https://keap.com/business-success-blog/marketing/email-marketing/send-better-emails\">Copy that’s about the reader, not the tool / platform / feature</a></li><li><a href=\"https://blogs.constantcontact.com/how-to-write-better-headlines/\">Copy that’s in the active voice</a></li><li><a href=\"https://copyhackers.com/2013/01/copywriting-tone-how-to/\">Copy that has a bit of voice but not too much</a></li><li><a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/blog/tabid/6307/bid/32606/the-9-must-have-components-of-compelling-email-copy.aspx\">Copy that talks about benefits before features</a></li><li><a href=\"https://litmus.com/blog/a-guide-to-animated-gifs-in-email\">A meaningful gif to illustrate the point the email is making</a></li><li><a href=\"https://copyhackers.com/2019/09/writing-ecommerce-emails/\">An image every few sentences &#8211; not too many or too few &#8211; to give the eye a chance to rest</a></li><li><a href=\"https://litmus.com/blog/click-tap-and-touch-a-guide-to-cta-best-practices\">A visible CTA</a></li><li><a href=\"https://www.copyblogger.com/call-to-action-buttons/\">A CTA that completes the phrase “I want to”</a></li><li><a href=\"https://www.campaignmonitor.com/blog/email-marketing/2019/06/improve-your-ctr-using-social-proof/\">Social proof</a></li><li><a href=\"https://www.convinceandconvert.com/email/guide-to-email-design-best-practices/\">Pleasing visual design</a></li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>And ^^ that email ^^ also adheres to <a href=\"https://10xlandingpages.com/\">essential copywriting rules</a>. That is, it follows one idea. It’s written for one reader (who, admittedly, could be clearer). It’s got one offer (which, admittedly, could be more irresistible). And it’s got one promise. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>These were emails that were based on email best practices. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Internally, our team was like, “Cool beans. Let’s test it.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>When we presented this email copy to the Prezi team, everyone was like, “Cool beans. Let’s test it.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>We were on board. They were on board. All stakeholders were on board. Everyone was perfectly fine with these emails going out the door. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Nobody was sure these emails would lose. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>That said, no one was sure they would win, either. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And of course…</p>\n\n\n\n<p>When the Prezi team tested our new email sequence against the control, our results were</p>\n\n\n\n<p>&#62;&#62;drumroll&#60;&#60;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>flat. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Like the emails had never happened. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Like Prezi had invested no time or resources in them. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Like Copyhackers had invested no time or resources in them. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Like they had never happened. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Except they HAD happened. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>There were 1000s of Prezi trial users who’d been exposed to them. Thousands of Prezi users who’d taken very little action after reading them. Not NO action. But very little action. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Prezi users were saying this about our copy: “Meh.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Worst thing is…</p>\n\n\n\n<p>A part of me thinks all of us &#8211; my team and the Prezi team &#8211; were thinking the same thing. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>We just didn’t say it. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2>Hindsight 20/20, sure &#8211; but here’s what I’ve started noticing about copy that doesn’t a) beat the control or b) lose to the control.</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>As soon as the world starts adopting a fashion trend, it’s already dead. Someone said that once. But you’ll have to trust me on that point because Google thinks I’m making it up. Evidently <a href=\"https://dilbert.com/search_results?terms=trends\">Dilbert</a> doesn’t have a comic for that yet. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>As soon as marketing recognizes a trend, it’s already dead. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Consider <a href=\"https://unbounce.com/design/branding-cartoon-illustration-design-trend/\">this now-famous Unbounce post</a> about the trend in illustrations on SaaS sites, which appears to have been first sparked by this tweet by <a href=\"https://twitter.com/jimmy_daly\">@jimmy_daly</a>:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1575323705246_Screen+Shot+2019-12-02+at+2.52.08+PM.png\" alt=\"Sweet from Jimmy Daly featuring illustrations in SaaS design.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>There’s never been a good idea that marketing hasn’t killed. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And I say that as a marketer. Who likes good ideas. Who’s had a few. And who’s killed many. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>As soon as we get wise to something, it’s already too late. As soon as you hear about it, it may already be time to consider it “inspiration” and use it to spark a better, fresher idea. Customers are already tuning it out. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>So with that in mind…</p>\n\n\n\n<p>I started looking around at the emails I was paying attention to. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Because my thought was this:</p>\n\n\n\n<h2>There’s gotta be something out there that’s not an email best practice yet but could inspire great emails.</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>(Yeah, I’m a swiper. You should swipe, too.) </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The first company that always springs to mind for me these days, when it comes to email, is <a href=\"https://www.stickermule.com/ca\">Sticker Mule</a>. Because their emails are all like this:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1576109111490_Screen+Shot+2019-12-11+at+5.05.01+PM.png\" alt=\"Sticker Mule email.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Basically, Sticker Mule emails break best practices in email copywriting. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>When I first started getting Sticker Mule emails, they annoyed me. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>They were so far from the vicinity of trying. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>A couple lines of text. No voice. Nothing about the customer. Such focus on discounts. Even the from name was doing things wrong! Best practices hold that your from name should be the name of a person &#8211; but Sticker Mule keeps sending me emails from Sticker Mule. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Annoying disregard for writing great emails. </p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>But then I noticed something:</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>I kept opening their emails. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>I kept clicking their text links. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And I kept ordering from them. </p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1576109278643_Screen+Shot+2019-12-11+at+5.07.25+PM.png\" alt=\"Screenshot of the money I spent with Sticker Mule in 2019, totalling nearly $1500.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Over the last 12 months, in fact, I’ve placed eight orders with Sticker Mule and spent nearly $1500. On stickers. And packaging to ship the stickers in. But mostly on stickers. Oh and buttons! Buttons were my new thing for about two months, thanks to emails like this:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1576109535178_Screen+Shot+2019-12-11+at+5.11.54+PM.png\" alt=\"Another Sticker Mule email.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Truth be told, I rarely even buy the thing they’re promoting in the email. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>I just click through, think about what I could do with whatever the promo is and then go shopping on Sticker Mule for something else that matches what I want to do now. </p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>But it’s not just Sticker Mule that’s doing cool &#8211; aka different &#8211; aka rule-breaking &#8211; stuff with email. </strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Perhaps you’re familiar with <a href=\"https://www.cbinsights.com/\">CB Insights</a>. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>CB Insights is a reporting company for investors / stakeholders in all things up-and-coming tech, like what’s trending in investing. They send a newsletter to report out what they’re seeing, finding, wondering, etc. It’s a great newsletter filled with data and dollars and charts and graphs. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Very serious stuff. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Money-making stuff. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Machine learning is involved.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Very serious stuff.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>But check this out. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>THIS is how the CEO of CB Insights signs off each of his newsletters:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1576109899763_Screen+Shot+2019-12-11+at+5.18.08+PM.png\" alt=\"Anand Sanwal's signature: &#34;I love you.&#34;\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>And it’s always been this way. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>At least, for as long as I’ve been subscribing. When I first started getting emails from CB Insights, it was Jan 5, 2016 &#8211; and this is how Anand signed that newsletter:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1576109999116_Screen+Shot+2019-12-11+at+5.19.08+PM.png\" alt=\"Another example of love in the signature.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>WTF?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>It’s amazing. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>“I love you.” “I still love you.” </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Quoi? </p>\n\n\n\n<p>That signature in what is essentially a finance newsletter?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>So while CEO Anand Sanwal is signing newsletters with the phrase most 1950s father figures couldn’t easily utter, if what TV taught me is true, which it definitely is… </p>\n\n\n\n<p>and while Sticker Mule throws a friendly eff u at all of us crazies with our email best practices… </p>\n\n\n\n<p>we’ve got this one other tricky style of email that was brought to my attention in 2019:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><a href=\"https://medium.com/@thatdeanjackson/an-amazing-9-word-email-that-revives-dead-leads-d1a1e97e5eea\">The 9-word email by Dean Jackson.</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p>The 9-word email is an email framework that one of my team members told me about after I sent this two-sentence email, promoting a new blog post, to the Copyhackers list. </p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577821746426_The+shortest+email+weve+ever+sent.png\" alt=\"An example of a short email Jo wrote.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>My team member was like, “You used the nine-word email.” </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And I was like, “What’s the nine-word email?” </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And she sent me to <a href=\"https://medium.com/@thatdeanjackson/an-amazing-9-word-email-that-revives-dead-leads-d1a1e97e5eea\">this article</a>. (There are other articles out there. And videos. Worth a Bing. &#60;&#8211; my husband keeps trying to make Bing happen, so that&#8217;s for him)</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Technically, the email I sent was not THE nine-word email; it was just a short email. Whatever the case, I found myself introduced to the nine-word email framework, which exists to revive dead leads. It does so using this formula:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Subject line: <br></strong> {recipient name}?</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Body: <br></strong> “Are you still looking for {the thing you’re selling}?” </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Which turns into something like this, for example:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Subject line: <br></strong> Elon?</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Body: <br></strong> “Are you still looking for smash-proof windshields?” </p>\n\n\n\n<p>That’s the whole thing. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And the results people share when they use the 9-word email are pretty incredible. I haven’t seen actual support for any of this, but if you’re cool with anecdotal evidence, people have used that formula to sell bigger-ticket items with longer sales cycles, like:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>Real estate</li><li>Vacations</li><li>Consulting services</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>The creator of the 9-word email, Dean Jackson lists these results for people who’ve used his formula:</p>\n\n\n\n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\"><p>A yacht broker sent “Are you still looking for a yacht?” and uncovered a $100 million dollar buyer. A Motorcycle jeans designer sold over $9000 in one week with a 9-word email. </p></blockquote>\n\n\n\n<p>Which brings us to this point:</p>\n\n\n\n<h2>There are more interesting things working in marketing than “best practices” expose us to.</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Using the above proceeds of my email swipe file review, I went back to the drawing board to work on a new round of Prezi onboarding emails. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>I tossed out everything we’d done in the “flat” round of onboarding emails except for: </p>\n\n\n\n<ol><li>the subject lines (which had good open rates) and </li><li>the basic flow of the emails &#8211; that is, which feature / benefit / outcome to talk about in the first email, then the second, then the third, etc. </li></ol>\n\n\n\n<p>In my writing, I swiped boldly from Sticker Mule… swiped lightly from CB Insights… and let the 9-word email keep me focused on a single point of relevance for the reader. In 45 minutes in my Macbook’s TextEdit program, I dashed out a brand new email sequence for Prezi, with this basic research question guiding the emails:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>What if every single thing we know about emails needs to be challenged? </em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Y’know, just a small question. Challenging the work I’ve done for the last 15 years. No big deal. No big whoop. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>With that and a new set of emails in hand, I reached out to Rita, Prezi’s lifecycle and growth marketing manager (who oversees email), to run my new copy by her. Thank God for Rita &#8211; she’s always open to experiments. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>After some convincing &#8211; not a lot but some &#8211; Rita and the Prezi team were on board with testing our new round of emails, which we called “The Bare Emails Experiment.” Here’s a representative email from the sequence:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1576110990592_Onboarding_SUL_Plus_Business_day_8.png\" alt=\"Prezi email - bare.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>These emails are:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>Intentionally stripped down visually &#8211; only a logo (to help with trust)</li><li>Short </li><li>Formatted simply, with one sentence per line and text links instead of buttons</li><li>Light in tone</li><li>Focused on instructing the trial user to do one thing</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>My favorite part? The sign off:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>You’re wonderful. </em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>It might be the kind of sign-off to make Anand Sanwal proud. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And best of all: some folks at Prezi and at Copyhackers did NOT like the sign-off. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Which is great. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>As I’ve started noticing, the copy that’s worth putting into the world &#8211; copy that’s worth putting in front of people who’d rather you send them cat gifs but who would, at the same time, rather you not send them cat gifs &#8211; is copy that does not get boardroom consensus. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2>Copy that’s worth publishing is copy that some of your team will like and others on your team will dislike. This “group discontent” is what we hypothesize to be the foundation of breakthrough or bust copy.</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Prezi tested our second round of emails. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And they beat the control with:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>An 18% lift in trial-to-subscribe rate, at 95% significance </li><li>A 71% lift in number of presentations created in 7 days after send, at 100% significance</li><li>A 93% lift in the number of presentations per user, at 100% significance</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>However, that was only for one of their segments. For two of their segments, we saw the same thing as the previous time: flat results. So we’re now working on two new sequences for those segments, with a new hypothesis about how to achieve a breakthrough &#8211; this one swiping from the winning segment while doubling-down on relevance for the segments. (More soon!) </p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Of course, what we’re learning is NOT that emails should be a mash-up of Sticker Mule emails, CB Insights sign-offs and the 9-word email in order to convert. </strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Rather, emails have to be <em>different from the norm</em> or from best practices to stand a chance of converting. The flipside is that differing from the norm or best practices also puts you at higher risk of negatively impacting conversions. Safe copy keeps results flat. Everything else introduces the risk of winning big or losing big. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Internally, we’ve started using the old direct-response term “breakthrough or bust” to describe the outcome of deviating from best practices, where the idea is that copy that stands a chance of converting may be a total breakthrough or a total bust… and you can’t tell before you launch it. You just have to be cool with the risk.  </p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>We once again tested what was developing into a recurring “breakthrough or bust hypothesis.” </strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>This time, the test was the trial-to-pro onboarding sequence for <a href=\"https://doodle.com/en/?utm_expid=.wXHXGBO5SoStV2KMwBRRog.1&#38;utm_referrer=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.google.com%2F\">Doodle</a>, an easy platform for scheduling meetings. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Here’s what a typical email from their Control sequence looked like. Have a read as if you’re a Doodle trial user:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577824255270_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+1.28.12+PM.png\" alt=\"A Doodle email.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>If you stripped away the design in the above Control email, you might end up with an email that was very much like the Prezi “bare emails” experiment. Just add an unexpectedly emotional sign-off, and you’d be set. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>We could have proposed Doodle test a bare version of the exact same copy they already had.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>But if we did that… could we say we were following our higher-level “breakthrough or bust” hypothesis? Would we have actual reason to believe the new version would convert? </p>\n\n\n\n<p>My opinion: nope. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>We’re trying to increase conversions. So going with a “sure thing” seems to be the safest way to get there. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>But that word “safe” is the first hint that you’re going down the wrong path. It takes you away from breakthrough or bust. It keeps you closer to flat results. Or so we’re learning. </p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Losing test after winning test after losing test, I have little reason to believe that the best next test to run is one based on copy that won elsewhere.</strong> Rather the best next copy test to run <em>may be</em> copy that’s the exact opposite of the copy that won elsewhere. If you repeat a winning test, are you running the risk of seeking a new best practice and, in turn, bringing the client / company closer to the point of mediocrity? I dunno. But it’s a question we’re always asking these days. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2>So what if we took the bare emails approach we’d used for Prezi… and swung the pendulum in the opposite direction for Doodle?</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>One of our philosophies at Copyhackers is that you should write for people who read. If you don’t want to write for people who read, you shouldn’t hire a copywriter and you certainly shouldn’t <a href=\"https://copyhackers.com/copywriting-agency/\">hire us</a>.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Doodle’s control emails were written not for people who read but for people who scan. Their emails were, we identified during our audit, too safe. Too timid. Some might say… a little scared. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Most emails are. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Hell, most copy is. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>So we decided, in the interest of writing breakthrough-or-bust copy &#8211; which by this time we were starting to affectionally call “BOB copy” &#8211; Bob is turning into a mascot around here BTW &#8211; that we should write emails that are quite definitely not safe. Certainly not timid. And 100% courageous &#8211; the opposite of scared. That might lead us to a breakthrough. Or a bust. But NOT flat results.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Our email conversion copywriter Nikki is one of the most courageous copywriters you’ll ever meet. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Here’s what an email Nikki wrote… and we proposed… and Doodle tested (Variation B) looked like. Give it a read like you’re a Doodle trial user:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577824350428_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+1.27.57+PM.png\" alt=\"A Doodle email.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>The first half of the Doodle onboarding sequence we wrote followed a similar style and format as the above email. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>It wasn’t until the second half, when we recommend Doodle start sending more sales emails, that our emails shortened up a bit and started to look more like this one, which is Sales Email #5:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577825677284_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+1.54.20+PM.png\" alt=\"A Doodle email.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>But it wasn’t just the style of the emails we recommended that challenged both email best practices and the Doodle control sequence. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>It was also the number of emails. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>(This is where things get particularly interesting for this study.) </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Doodle’s control sequence was 4 emails long. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Ours was 16. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Yup, we recommended 4x the emails to Doodle. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>To summarize the changes we recommended that we believed to be BOB:</p>\n\n\n\n<ol><li>Four times the emails</li><li>Five times the sales emails</li><li>A near-daily send frequency, compared to intermittent sending with the control</li><li>Unexpected subject lines</li><li>Subject lines that mimicked those used when scheduling meetings, which some could argue is a dark UX practice even though it was an important part of the message and not dark in intent</li><li>Longer left-aligned copy with a narrative style</li><li>A signature from someone at Doodle</li></ol>\n\n\n\n<p>We also wrote an email from Time itself. Not from Doodle. From Time, the thing Doodle helps you save:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577826102822_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+2.01.10+PM.png\" alt=\"A Doodle email.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>And then there was this classic subject line, which helped this email earn the highest unsubscribe rate Doodle had ever seen: &#8220;My coworkers hate me.&#8221; See it here:</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577826123226_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+2.00.31+PM.png\" alt=\"A Doodle email.\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>So that’s four times the emails sent. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Way more sales-focused emails. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Some tone “problems.” </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Some long-ass copy. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And the results:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>63.9% lift in purchases of the Pro product</li><li>18.3% lift in purchases of the Starter product</li><li>Double to triple the unsubscribes </li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>But those lifts were largely for US-based trial users. The Brits and the Aussies responded better to the control sequence. As did non-English speakers.</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>A note from Val Geisler, our peer reviewer for this article and an email expert:</strong> <em>Too many marketers think unsubs are a bad thing. Unsubs are GOOD in this case (and pretty much every case) because people who don’t want to hear from you shouldn’t hear from you. </em></p>\n\n\n\n<h2>Ready for something that’s quite a bit more interesting? </h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Because we’ve all seen stories of conversion lifts… and because this whole post is about how you can’t just repeat what someone did, hoping it’s a best practice, and expect to see the same results…</p>\n\n\n\n<p>If you’re starting to think about how to write BOB emails or BOB copy for your organization, you’re going to need to get ready for… reactions. </p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>Allow me to illustrate:</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Exactly zero trial users replied to a single email in the Doodle control sequence. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Can you guess how many replied to the emails in Variation B / the BOB sequence we wrote?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Try 107 replies from Doodle Pro trial users… and 204 replies from Doodle Starter trial users.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Imagine explaining that to your success team. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Imagine getting the reluctant okay from your marketing team and the boardroom to move forward with a BOB email sequence… only to have CS call an emergency meeting when BOB launches to deal with 300+ emails from customers that include content like:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>Please stop. </em></p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>Your emails are hilarious.</em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>and </p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>Doodle is great. Trying to shame people into buying your product? Poor. </em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Even a paid conversion lift of &#62;60% couldn’t keep your team from demanding you take the emails down. Which almost happened in this case. But instead we removed the particularly problematic email &#8211; which is the one with the subject line “My coworkers hate me” you saw above &#8211; and made some other technical modifications to deal with people getting more emails than they should have. Conversion rate stayed high. And angry emails went away. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Does it make you nervous, the idea of getting 300x the email replies you used to get? </p>\n\n\n\n<p>It should make us all just as nervous to get ZERO replies. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>But it doesn’t. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>That part doesn’t make most marketers nervous. We try to keep our opens around 20%. And we use that as our primary measure of engagement. “People are still opening. We must be doing fine.”</p>\n\n\n\n<p>You are doing fine. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Just fine.  </p>\n\n\n\n<h2>Y’know that feeling when you have no feeling about something?</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>I don’t think I’m overly ornery when I say that most copy out there is meh at best. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Great copy lives on the edge of the blade. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>And that’s why so few people write or publish it.  </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Great copy is uncomfortable. A few people around the boardroom table will love it, but the vast majority will shoot it down fast. Some think it will be the breakthrough a company needs. Others think it will tank conversions. If you were to ask the room to vote on whether they think it would beat the control, no one would say yes. Even the copywriter who wrote it. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>THAT’S potentially great copy. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>It’s also potentially shit copy. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>There’s a name for it now. It’s called BOB copy. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>It’s not “fake news” headlines or clickbait. It’s also not copy that’s filled with swearwords. Or takes a hard right when the market goes left. Or is about sex when it shouldn’t be about sex. Or is anything Cambridge Analytica would have signed off on &#8211; gross manipulation disguised as &#8220;persuasion.” </p>\n\n\n\n<p>BOB copy makes you react. Makes you feel something. And for those huge marketing sins, it’s hated by the vast majority of the boardroom. A boardroom has never turned out great copy. Good copy, yes. The kind of copy your designer whips up while sketching out your new home page, yes. But not great copy.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>You have the theory behind BOB copy. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Now add this checklist to the mix to help you identify if your copy is likely to be a total breakthrough or a total bust (and importantly: you can&#8217;t be sure which one of those it’ll be). </p>\n\n\n\n<h2>This is our current but growing checklist for writing breakthrough-or-bust copy.</h2>\n\n\n\n[ ] It boldly breaks best practices for the medium or channel. <br>\n[ ] It boldly breaks best practices for copywriting for that medium or channel.<br>\n[ ] It boldly breaks best practices in design / design conventions for that medium or channel.<br>\n[ ] It leverages insights from data without said data choking or stifling potentially fruitful ideas.<br>\n[ ] It boldly runs counter to the Control. <br>\n[ ] It is still technically on-brand for the company. <br>\n[ ] You are scared to present it. But you know it ticks the above boxes.<br>\n[ ] You are certain many people will hate it. But you know it ticks the above boxes.<br>\n[ ] You know there is a WHY for every practice challenged.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2>If best practices breed mediocre results, Joanna, should I even learn how to write copy… or just throw wild guesses at the page?</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>There’s a rule in writing: you don’t get to break the rules of grammar until you know the rules of grammar. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>My take is it’s the same in the copywriting world: you don’t get to break copywriting best practices until or unless you know those best practices. How would you know which best practices to challenge if you don’t know the best practices to begin?</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Oh, and keep a swipe file. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>~jo </p>\n<script elivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn99@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-39-01-235_b048489633b12299.webp","https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1576109999116_Screen+Shot+2019-12-11+at+5.19.08+PM.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn50@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-39-08-659_83a8b74e07dd0335.webp","https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577821746426_The+shortest+email+weve+ever+sent.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn90@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-39-09-020_8a6e931b877ed356.webp","https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1576110990592_Onboarding_SUL_Plus_Business_day_8.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn58@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-39-04-269_12b461d2675daa3e.webp","https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577824255270_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+1.28.12+PM.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn42@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-39-07-935_9b7fbcacf2aea0fb.webp","https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577824350428_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+1.27.57+PM.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn38@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-39-09-796_c86db6157f6d379a.webp","https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577825677284_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+1.54.20+PM.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn45@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-39-02-236_9ad7508685dd5cdf.webp","https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577826102822_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+2.01.10+PM.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn25@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-39-02-916_dbcdc2afbc9228f3.webp","https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_CD7DE7A5C9380F5D9F12F495522675BCEAC6EA146880A70D45A4E4BFBBA6CE84_1577826123226_Screen+Shot+2019-12-31+at+2.00.31+PM.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn81@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-39-08-186_02415e244ac6c5ed.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598323135044},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:38:55","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:38:55","title":"What Is UX Copywriting?","link":"https://copyhackers.com/?p=24630","description":"\n<p><em>UX copywriting, or user-experience copywriting, is the act of writing and structuring copy that moves digital users, like visitors and customers, toward accomplishing a goal in an intuitive way.&#160;</em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Let’s start by viciously ripping that term &#8211; UX copywriting &#8211; apart.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2>What is user experience (UX)?</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>UX describes how, when and why people interact with a product or service &#8211; and how they feel about their experience. If you wear clothes, eat food and have a roof over your head, you’re having multiple user experiences every single second.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Let’s use an article of clothing as an example. Socks!&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>If you wear socks, you’re a user of that product, and your <em>experience</em> starts long before your toes get warm ‘n’ cozy.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>First, you realize you need socks. Maybe, like me, you’ve got somewhat of a holey sock epidemic and almost every time you put on a pair, a circle of skin stares right back at ya. So you start shopping for new socks. You compare your options. You find some you want. You buy them. You unpackage them. And finally, you put them on.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Almost anything can contribute to a good or bad user experience. Like, if I ordered ankle socks online and received thigh highs instead. </p>\n\n\n\n\n<p>Bad experience.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>All user experiences are affected by things like:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>The reason you decide to use or purchase something in the first place</li><li>The time of year and time of day you use the product</li><li>Any friction or pleasant surprises you encounter</li><li>How you feel throughout the whole journey</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>UX applies to both physical and digital products but for the rest of this post, I’ll be talking about digital.&#160;In digital marketing, we can optimize UX by understanding our audience and creating brand and product interactions that are:</p>\n\n\n\n<ol><li>Intuitive</li><li>Relevant to people’s needs</li></ol>\n\n\n\n<p>And we can use copy to guide them toward a user experience that satisfies both their goals &#8211; and ours.</p>\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n<h2>Copywriting is all about purpose and persuasion</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>When copywriting, we write with a goal in mind. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Always.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>And to achieve that goal, we take the desires people already have and show them how to fulfill them with a product or service. (I’m paraphrasing the legendary Eugene Schwartz and his concept of mass desire here.)&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Ultimately, copy should compel people to take an action. Without a word wasted.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2>How to make your copy more UX-friendly</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>UX copywriting is the act of writing and structuring copy that moves people toward accomplishing a goal in an intuitive way.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>You’ve seen this in lots of headlines, microcopy and button copy. I’ll show you an example of each.</p>\n\n\n\n<h3>UX-friendly headlines</h3>\n\n\n\n<p>I’m new to conversion copywriting, so I’m eager to learn everything I can. This means I’m on an endless hunt for books, courses and training to better my skills.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>And even though I’m actively looking for guidance, I probably won’t buy a conversion copywriting kit from some guy in an alley. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Or blindly follow advice I find online. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>I want to make sure I’m getting copywriting resources from someone I trust.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Like Copyhackers.</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_28F3A6DCBFCED9B1A5AAD72B59648BCCD0DB1DA86A12C7C0507FD716989622C5_1572829173502_Screen+Shot+2019-11-03+at+5.59.10+PM.png\" alt=\"Example of UX copywriting in a heading\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>When I’m greeted with the Copyhackers headline above, I’m snagged. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The headline acknowledges where I’m at in my journey as a fresh-to-the-biz conversion copywriter and offers something I need &#8211; the essentials.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>This headline contributes to a positive user experience by:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>Keeping the copy succinct</li><li>Using bold formatting to emphasize the benefit of this page</li><li>Directly relating to me and what I do by using the term conversion copywriting &#8211; so I’m keen to continue reading and eventually sign up to receive the Conversion Copywriting 101 course.</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<h3>UX-friendly microcopy</h3>\n\n\n\n<p>I write Facebook ads, so I have a vested interest in how social media advertising engages people.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://paper-attachments.dropbox.com/s_28F3A6DCBFCED9B1A5AAD72B59648BCCD0DB1DA86A12C7C0507FD716989622C5_1572884305560_Screen+Shot+2019-11-04+at+9.17.44+AM.png\" alt=\"Example of UX copywriting in microcopy\"/></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>When I see this post, I’m instantly intrigued by the title. Below that, I notice the name of the author &#8211; Sarah Sal &#8211; as well as the microcopy: “19 Min Read.”&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The estimated reading time answers an important question:&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li><em>How much time will I need to </em><em>set aside to </em><em>read this?</em></li></ul>\n\n\n\n<p>Once I know how long it will take, I can decide if there’s enough cushion in my schedule at this exact moment or if I should save it for later.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>So even though I may not read the post right away, the microcopy gives me the details I need to plan to read it another day, which still brings me one step closer to accomplishing the main goal of reading the article.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<h3>UX-friendly button copy</h3>\n\n\n\n<p>The copy on any button should persuade people to click it because they believe it will make their life better.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The button text above makes that click extra enticing by offering me front-of-the-line-access to Copyhackers’ guest blogging course.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>In addition to that, the button copy creates a good user experience by:</p>\n\n\n\n<ul><li>Centring the text so my eyes are focused right where the button should be clicked</li><li>Using a different colour from the rest of the text, so I understand it has a different purpose</li><li>Formatting the word ‘all’ in upper case which disrupts the sentence case pattern and stops me from skimming</li></ul>\n\n\n\n<h4>Steve Krug said it best&#8230;</h4>\n\n\n\n<p><em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.ca/Dont-Make-Me-Think-Usability/dp/0321344758\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\"Don’t make me think (opens in a new tab)\">Don’t make me think</a>.</em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Whether clicking a button or scrolling down a page, people want clarity. And they want to make decisions they feel good about.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<h2>How to make reader journeys easier with UX copywriting (so we can all accomplish our goals)</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Our job as copywriters and digital marketers is first to listen to what people want and need, then illustrate the easiest ways to accomplish their goals &#8211; without any distractions or wasted words.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>We couldn’t do this without:</p>\n\n\n\n<ol><li><strong><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\"Listening to our audience (opens in a new tab)\" href=\"https://copyhackers.com/2018/05/how-to-listen/\" target=\"_blank\">Listening to our audience</a>.</strong> Understanding their hopes. Fears. Desires. Frustrations. These insights are essential for good copy <em>and</em> good user experience.&#160;</li><li><strong><a href=\"https://www.interaction-design.org/literature/article/principle-of-consistency-and-standards-in-user-interface-design\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" aria-label=\"Adopting web conventions (opens in a new tab)\">Adopting web conventions</a>.</strong> On landing pages, sales pages and emails, people expect buttons to look like buttons. They expect hyperlinks to be underlined. Headlines to be bolded. Messages to be matched. If we don’t follow best practices, we risk confusing (and losing) our audience.&#160;</li></ol>\n\n\n\n<p>Both clients and customers have important goals. Copywriters find where those align so they can show people <em>how</em> to achieve their desires. Good UX copy makes the journey effortless.&#160;</p>\n\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n\nwindow.rmpanda = window.rmpanda || {};\nwindow.rmpanda.wpvars = window.rmpanda.wpvars || {};\n\nvar debug
